The bad news is the specialist had nothing to add, except this:&nbsp;</span><span style="font-family: arial;">"Give it another three months, and we\'ll take a checkpoint."&nbsp;</span></div><div dir="auto"><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div dir="auto"><span style="font-family: arial;">That might sound dismissive or flabby, but to me it\'s significant. Experts are often able to pull accurate numbers out of the air.</span></div><div dir="auto"><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div dir="auto"><span style="font-family: arial;">Her choice of "three months" may seem like a guess, but it\'s based on her informed intution:</span>&nbsp;</div><div dir="auto"><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Mike\'s meds are likely to need three more months to fully do their job</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Multiple studies happening right now could have some results in three months&nbsp;</span></li></ol><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><b>This means settling in and staying patient.</b></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">And her other intuition was counter-intuitive:</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">She said to chase the depression, not the fatigue.&nbsp;At first blush, it sounds counter-intuitive because his fatigue is even higher than his depression. But it also makes sense, because the depression may be easier to treat.&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><b>This means further discussion with his meds doctor.</b>&nbsp;</span></div></div>'}, {'url': 'http://www.longcovidmind.info/2022/11/11-01-caring-about-others-when-in-crisis.html', 'title': '22-11-01 Caring about others when in crisis', 'content': '<p></p><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;"><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgdEpNnsBeNjhkkrcRGLyxMrMSDvcTyjGJb1eJFp5GGRk-xOm3y_QRjK-hsQ0UEBvSbvjZ9UiM3pqkS3dSfZbrpDiQ82EYjxRV5zobJOXe65K4Y2KjWxsLaW_XQh_m9vSe-kOMqwz8F5PD4hTlKmfR2Andri7rJiHCzif1RSBhLBf6W8EBNMYr7pMlVUg/s315/images.jpg" style="clear: right; float: right; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: 1em;"><img border="0" data-original-height="160" data-original-width="315" height="160" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgdEpNnsBeNjhkkrcRGLyxMrMSDvcTyjGJb1eJFp5GGRk-xOm3y_QRjK-hsQ0UEBvSbvjZ9UiM3pqkS3dSfZbrpDiQ82EYjxRV5zobJOXe65K4Y2KjWxsLaW_XQh_m9vSe-kOMqwz8F5PD4hTlKmfR2Andri7rJiHCzif1RSBhLBf6W8EBNMYr7pMlVUg/s1600/images.jpg" width="315" /></a></div>This condition we\'re dealing with is traumatic. When I\'m in some kind of trauma or crisis, whether it\'s acute or long-term, there\'s something I do: I continue to reach out and care about the concerns of those I\'m closest to.&nbsp;</span></div><p></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">And they do the same for me. My closest loved ones manage to still be in my support circle, even when they\'re having their own trauma. A few ways it\'s good for me, and I imagine it\'s good for them too:</span></p><p></p><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s healthy for me to get out of my own pain&nbsp;&nbsp;</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s good for my perspective -- others have crises and problems too</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It helps me to remain close to them when I stay in their lives</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It helps me to remind them of my care and concern for them, even when I\'m in pain too</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It sets a tone for myself of "bad is bad." It\'s not a competition.</span></li></ol><div><span style="font-family: arial;">It makes me happy that I can do this. And that I have a close circle of loved ones who do this for me. I\'m so lucky. And of course, these exchanges don\'t have to be 50-50. Sometimes they\'re 70-30. Other times they might be 30-70.&nbsp;</span><span style="font-family: arial;">Even Mike has been a support to me through taking care of him.&nbsp;&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Some of how my support folks have helped me:</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">You have listened and taken interest, even though you have your own stuff</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">You don\'t rate and rank your traumas against mine. You accept that "bad is bad"</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">You don\'t vie for who has the worst problem, except sometimes in a generous direction lol!</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">My mentally and physically handicapped son is my role model of giving while getting. He receives services, but also provides services to the handicapped.&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">As persons in trauma, it\'s good to do these things. We need to try and do some kind of exchange.&nbsp;</span><span style="font-family: arial;">I don\'t claim to do this well all the time, but it\'s something I value.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s not good to forget others\' pain, or they will indeed burn out as support providers. We need to be open to them even while we\'re in our own trauma. T</span><span style="font-family: arial;">here are small and loving ways to do that.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">And on the flip side, we need to marginalize those who cannot do the same for us. It\'s too painful to receive zero. Perhaps they\'re not much of a friend at all.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><span style="font-size: 19.2px;">Support my Long Covid Mind Facebook group by joining</span><br style="font-size: 19.2px;" /><a href="https://www.facebook.com/groups/longcovidmind" style="font-size: 19.2px;">Click here to see it</a></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><br /></div><p></p>'}, {'url': 'http://www.longcovidmind.info/2022/10/10-27-good-days-and-bad-days.html', 'title': '22-10-28 Good days and bad days', 'content': '<p><a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiGqdp676Xov3rVM7iO6eCb-qvmYwhSnrnCslxIfcI5x32jjlPfIfbNALZt_XYmZ4gFya62XKBImK-5Re3cYVXfcoM9CIUTJSiXeSlTB7v0dOYYLNhVPMe8WH3AFcnxcPtFmIdIh7NL7TMgksVYEjuj2XDHhqqNw4A_bBf7vOmpXkhMBYxOnr1BlQxmhA/s1024/311580398_10229181001368796_8481388834518871408_n.jpg" style="clear: right; float: right; font-size: 32px; font-weight: 700; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: 1em; text-align: center;"><img border="0" data-original-height="1024" data-original-width="843" height="400" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiGqdp676Xov3rVM7iO6eCb-qvmYwhSnrnCslxIfcI5x32jjlPfIfbNALZt_XYmZ4gFya62XKBImK-5Re3cYVXfcoM9CIUTJSiXeSlTB7v0dOYYLNhVPMe8WH3AFcnxcPtFmIdIh7NL7TMgksVYEjuj2XDHhqqNw4A_bBf7vOmpXkhMBYxOnr1BlQxmhA/w329-h400/311580398_10229181001368796_8481388834518871408_n.jpg" width="329" /></a></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">"I have good days and bad days." This is what I hear from folks who have ME/CFS.&nbsp;&nbsp;</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">But by his own self-report, Mike\'s still having only bad days. There\'s little to no fluctuation from one day to the next. Rather, he is doing minimal, necessary, functioning each day, and that\'s all.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Will we know what to do when a good day comes? How will we recognize it? How does pacing work when one is having a good day?</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">I met someone this week who has had ME/CFS for many years, and has those ups and downs.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">For her, boredom is a good indicator. On her bad days, she never feels bored. But if she\'s lying on the couch one day and her brain registers boredom, she might be heading toward a few good days.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">When that happens, she makes careful attempts to do a bit more, such as make her own lunch. Sometimes her body is still not able, but other times, it is. She paces accordingly.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Mike says he\'s not been bored once yet. But that description is a help to us. Management of this illness involves self-awareness under a microscope.&nbsp;</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">His first recognition of boredom will be a tiny step up. But even looking forward to boredom is a tiny step up. It adds to our hope.</span></p>'}, {'url': 'http://www.longcovidmind.info/2022/10/10-27-know-even-one-fact-about-lc-and.html', 'title': '22-10-27 Know one fact; become mighty', 'content': '<p><span style="font-family: arial;"></span></p><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;"><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhfyDMbNIWnUFVqhWKRltCWw5ABGqCTyg8pcbdpD4lLoTcP_L2J2dPvQiHsNl-y3ksbgiSyM8DQfxh7WiZMO7j8QYtHlX7puRB6AMivx1Vv9tsNA7ELMs0NiaWi8RAsBWEGuz8eIPT8do8IS8W4AzmTzDLPevQyeePNdNzrc7vGR-RA5pRN-ffBy77J0g/s450/new.jpg" style="clear: right; float: right; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: 1em;"><img border="0" data-original-height="338" data-original-width="450" height="240" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhfyDMbNIWnUFVqhWKRltCWw5ABGqCTyg8pcbdpD4lLoTcP_L2J2dPvQiHsNl-y3ksbgiSyM8DQfxh7WiZMO7j8QYtHlX7puRB6AMivx1Vv9tsNA7ELMs0NiaWi8RAsBWEGuz8eIPT8do8IS8W4AzmTzDLPevQyeePNdNzrc7vGR-RA5pRN-ffBy77J0g/s320/new.jpg" width="320" /></a></div>I\'m thinking maybe we\'d all go crazy if we had to understand every medical condition and disability in existence. Even the medical community is unable to study them all.</span></div><p></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Instead, what we tend to do mainly is to learn about an illness or disability when it affects us or a loved one directly. I totally agree with that strategy.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">I\'m the first to admit I\'d like to stay ignorant about most conditions. And in fact, I\'m not even learning about all of Long Covid. I\'m zooming in mostly on Mike\'s particular symptoms.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">So why bother to know anything at all about Long Covid? Why is it so powerful to just have a few facts or an anecdote at the ready? And why is it so important?</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">I\'ll summarize first by saying Long Covid is the perfect storm of all the worst circumstances:</span></p><p></p><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Each patient gets a selection of symptoms from a range of over two hundred assorted, seemingly unrelated symptoms. (The NY Times reports the number as 203.)</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">The CDC only announced the new diagnosis in July of 2022. It\'s called "PASC" but that hasn\'t done much good yet because of item #1 above. It\'s not specific enough yet.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Most of the medical community is still in an information vacuum and so are all of us.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Information holes are always filled by misinformation and assumptions. It\'s human nature, and sadly, that fact is true of care providers, too.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Cultural norms and constraints can serve as a deterrent to science and good information.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Denial is an even more universal deterrent -- it protects our sense of safety.&nbsp;&nbsp;</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Many ongoing studies of Long Covid have not yet published their results. It takes time.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It is still unknown who might get Long Covid, so we have to assume that means everyone.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">The mildness or even lack of Covid symptoms doesn\'t rule out risk of Long Covid.</span></li></ol><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Thus even sharing one fact can help spread good information. Or one anecdote about someone you know with Long Covid. You don\'t have to know much to know far more than most. Some folks will hear it, especially if they already know and respect you.&nbsp;</span><span style="font-family: arial;">&nbsp;</span></div><p></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">In the world of illness and disability, advocacy has long been expected from those who have the specific conditions. It\'s called self-advocacy, and it\'s been extolled for sound reasons, but it\'s not easy. They and their loved ones are already bogged down and limited by the condition. They need help from friends.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">So there\'s a tiny role any of us can play, to help fill in the vacuum, even if it\'s just spreading around a good article or two. Or sharing one fact or anecdote. And sharing proper terminology such as "Post Covid Syndrome" or "PASC" (which stands for this mouthful: P</span><span style="font-family: arial;">ost Acute Sequelae of Covid-19</span><span style="font-family: arial;">). The term "Long Covid" is still used, but "Post Covid Syndrome" is better.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">In case you\'re not persuaded, here\'s my closing thought: because of Long Covid,</span><span style="font-family: arial;">&nbsp;families are being torn apart right this very minute. Spouses are leaving, relatives are going no-contact, and doctors are misdiagnosing and even dropping patients. Sufferers of this illness are still being misunderstood, maligned, devalued, and accused of laziness. I know these things because I\'m involved with Survivor Corps. I read their stories every day.&nbsp;</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Don\'t even get me started with the inequities that have resulted. I know it\'s behind a paywall, but here\'s the New York Times predicting it:&nbsp;<a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2022/03/29/health/long-covid-black.html" target="_blank">Experts warn of racial disparities in the diagnosis and treatment of long Covid</a>.</span>&nbsp;</p>'}, {'url': 'http://www.longcovidmind.info/2022/10/10-22-yup-pem-has-happened.html', 'title': "22-10-23 It's known as PEM", 'content': '<div style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;"><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: left;"><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiZMjfKYizCyyThV4ld2PyeLdPNNIvZtKoGklXvlV0h_mYI18n2xCksbGV7HxjOW_IlpxtEokv9DRAyuSoTeA4dRlN5FOHFTw9BOgovChdW7Tq9l96bHLkcLkhscDARgkeHMnK3gRjWyLJtIK3-H0vNYgvvpdI3b5zd-ScKzGMUQhm2iFSnKCXMW9kjAQ/s600/pasc.jpg" style="clear: right; float: right; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: 1em;"><img border="0" data-original-height="438" data-original-width="600" height="234" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiZMjfKYizCyyThV4ld2PyeLdPNNIvZtKoGklXvlV0h_mYI18n2xCksbGV7HxjOW_IlpxtEokv9DRAyuSoTeA4dRlN5FOHFTw9BOgovChdW7Tq9l96bHLkcLkhscDARgkeHMnK3gRjWyLJtIK3-H0vNYgvvpdI3b5zd-ScKzGMUQhm2iFSnKCXMW9kjAQ/s320/pasc.jpg" width="320" /></a></div>Long Covid causes ME/CFS (formerly known as Chronic Fatigue Syndrome) the study of which has been underfunded for decades but is now getting renewed attention due to the explosion of Long Covid.</div></span></div><div style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">ME/CFS has to be managed with "pacing." Was probably just a placebo effect."</span><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span><div><span style="font-family: arial;">"You see? You responded to a placebo, so you weren\'t sick after all."</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">"I won\'t try that. It will probably just have a placebo effect."</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">"Placebo effect means you are easily influenced."&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Things folks say about the placebo effect fail to make sense.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Why do we devalue the placebo effect? If it works, it works. But as a culture, we tend to relegate placebo effect to weak-mindedness or lack of scientific proof.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">In doing so, we devalue its place in the world of medicine.</span><span style="font-family: arial;">&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Since the placebo effect was first demonstrated in the 18th century, it has been simultaneously marginalized and acknowledged for its effectiveness.&nbsp;&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">When we bought that weighted blanket, I became interested in the placebo effect. Something about the blanket appealed to Mike so much that he looked forward to going to bed. For someone with a sleep disorder, this is significant.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">A weighted blanket is reported to work well for night waking, but we were trying to use it for sleep onset. So I wondered if it might work anyway. That got me thinking...perhaps placebo effect is especially effective when it comes to sleep. After all, when we lie awake, we get increasingly stressed about not being alseep. If we believe something will work, we relax.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">So I pounded google, and discovered something interesting -- placebo effect gets more serious and detailed attention as it applies to sleep disorders.&nbsp;&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span It\'s likely his hunger will return to normal in time. Wellbutrin can lead to increased anxiety, trouble sleeping and lack of hunger, but can fade once your body acclimates.&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Wellbutrin is a unique drug because it is an NDRI which works differently than SSRIs/SRNIs which work on Serotonin&nbsp;(this is how much of psychiatric treatment of depression has been based on for many decades, and&nbsp;actually there\'s new evidence that none of it has been much better than placebo pills). NDRI is a new-er class of drugs that works on&nbsp;Norepinephrine and Dopamine&nbsp;instead.&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Other drugs in the same class are used for ADHD treatment because they can be helpful for focus and energy, so this route makes sense as it could also help with focus and brain fog. Research is still emerging about it but they seem to have less side effects than SSRIs.&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Things to look out for is because it\'s a depression treatment that boosts energy, sometimes people can see increases in suicidal ideation, which is the big thing to report if that shows up.&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s also possible he might see the anxiety spike back up, at least temporarily. This med route makes sense to me though. And withdrawal is really minimal compared to many other depression treatment drugs so it\'s very safe to come off of if it doesn\'t pan out.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">You may have already tried these things for sleep too butMelatonin and Magnesium (especially magnesium glycinate, used in combination with melatonin) can help regulate sleep cycles. 5-HTP also helps with the production of melatonin. If he\'s tried melatonin and found it useless, a higher dose might help but it could also be an issue of magnesium deficiency which is common in older adults.&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Figured I\'d suggest some supplements since you won\'t want to be experimenting with other meds while he\'s adjusting to a med. If he\'s still on gabapentin, I would take that one at nighttime since it&nbsp;can make you sleepy. I would also highly recommend taking CBD as part of a high-CBD low-THC combo -- CBD is just not very effective on its own as much as the CBD industry really wants people to believe it.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br style="background-color: white; color: #222222; font-size: small;" /></span></div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br />\nComments? It wasn\'t obvious to me.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Even with the visibility of her wheelchair, it\'s not easy for people to understand. What is her life really like? But the same question goes for a broader group:</span></div><div><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s not easy for people to understand chronic disability.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s not easy for people to understand chronic illness.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s not easy for people to understand invisible disability.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s not easy for people to understand invisible illness.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s not easy for people to understand mental illness.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s not easy for people to understand a new, unstudied condition.</span></li></ol><div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">And even if you\'re on the inside of one or more of these, everyone is unique in their individual stew of symptoms, so it\'s still not easy to understand, even if you think you do.</span></div></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">It seems like Post Covid illness crosses all six. Our support team (everyone reading this blog post, plus some others) are already great at these tips. But it does help to read it in words that are fleshed out. I\'m still learning too.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">And here\'s a few of my own suggestions, tailored to Mike:</span></div><div><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Suspend all knowledge you might already have; this illness is too individual and too new.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Cultivate curiosity and stay open-minded; be willing to self-correct assumptions.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Avoid both extreme positivity and extreme negativity. Even avoid too many words of encouragment.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Refrain from drawing conclusions.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Recognize that this is not a big emotional thing. It\'s a daily thing.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">This illness as so many different moving parts; if you hear of a "step forward," be open to hear about its often accompanying "step back."</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Finding commonalities between you and the patient is nice, but use it judiciously. The population of patients with ME/CFS has grown considerably in the past few years as a result of Covid-19, resulting in a resurgence of interest.&nbsp;<span style="text-align: center;">&nbsp;</span></span></div><div><span style="background-color: white; color: #050505; font-size: 15px; white-space: pre-wrap;"><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></span></div><div><span style="background-color: white; color: #050505; font-size: 15px; white-space: pre-wrap;"><span style="font-family: arial;">And better studies can be done now, again because of Long Covid. Past studies of ME/CFS relied on small populations of patients, all sick at different times, and who had been infected by a wide range of different viruses. In complete contrast, we now have a larger population, all sick at the same time, and they all had some form of Covid-19. </span></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span><span><span style="background-color: white; color: #050505; font-family: arial; font-size: 15px; white-space: pre-wrap;">Further and newer studies may show slightly different results, but perhaps drastically different results are unlikely. </span></span></span></div><div><span><span><span style="background-color: white; color: #050505; font-family: arial; font-size: 15px; white-space: pre-wrap;"><br /></span></span></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">A final thought about that second chart: Obviously, if a patient has more than one of these diseases or disabilities, they might swap their severity rankings. Background: he has had hand tremors for decades, which got so bad in the last year that he had non-invasive, ultrasound brain surgery to correct the right hand which was worse than the left. We were content with that because his right hand is tremor-free since the surgery. But now the left hand tremors have worsened.&nbsp;<br /><br />Tremors are common to post COVID-19 conditions, and some experts suspect it\'s related to autonomic nervous system dysfunction. This explanation fits Mike because all of his symptoms so far have been neurological.<br /><br /></span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Delayed Sleep-Wake Phase Disorder (a Circadian Rhythm Disorder).&nbsp; Sleep onset has moved to 3:30 am or later, and now it\'s followed by early waking instead of sleeping late. We\'ve tried the usual remedies; nothing has budged his sleep onset time so far, but we\'re still trying.&nbsp;&nbsp;<br /><br />It is a known fact that sleep onset delays are common to ME/CFS, which Mike has as one of his post Covid illnesses.<br /><br />I recently reported some remedies he\'s embarking on (alongside his current prescriptions) but we just started:<br /><br /></span></li><ol><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Weighted blanket -- reportedly takes at least a week to get used to it and then another three weeks for it to start working, but Mike loved it instantly, so that\'s encouraging</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Magnesium and Melatonin which work together</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">CBD (in a 20 to 1 ratio with THC)<br /><br /></span></li></ol><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Sleep Deprived most days: Due to shortened sleep, he is now tired a lot, along with but separate from the fatigue. He is able to distinguish between the fatigue and the tiredness.<br /><br /></span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Appetite: It&nbsp; has declined to zero. He now has to remember to eat at least one meal a day.<br /><br /></span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Dizziness in the morning: We think it\'s from the Melatonin, as that happens to some folks who take Melatonin</span></li></ol><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div></div><h2 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial; font-size: small;">Update on 10-10-22:</span></h2><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Last night Mike fell asleep at a normal time, and without alcohol. This is a stunning event. So the most likely causes of a better sleep onset could be the melatonin and the magnesium or it could be the weighted blanket or maybe it could be the probiotics (only because the probiotics are also new) or maybe the fatigue being higher yesterday and the day before. He said his brain fog has lifted since yesterday. He specifically mentioned that his focus and concentration are restored. In addition, his motivation is improved. He believes these changes to be due to the Wellbutrin, even though it\'s only been four days since he started it.&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Interestingly, he still needs to rest a lot between activities, and he still rates the fatigue at a 70. His explanation is that he likens the effect of Wellbutrin to the effect of Sudafed when you have a cold -- it gives you some motivation to get things done even though you don\'t feel well.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><h4><span style="font-family: arial;">Some downsides and one neutral change in the last few days:</span></h4><div><ol><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He\'s been consistently falling alseep very late, which could be exacerbated by the Wellbutrin and the lack of alcohol.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He is waking up too early and therefore is not well rested in the last two days.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">His appetite had been gradually disappearing in the last month. But in the last few days he has no appetite at all, and so he now has to eat on a schedule. He hasn\'t lost his sense of taste, though, and he does enjoy his meals. He just doesn\'t get hungry.&nbsp; The doctor hasn\'t been concerned.</span></li></ol><div><h1><span style="background-color: #f4cccc; font-family: arial;">The Original Post</span></h1></div></div><div><span style="background-color: #f4cccc; font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div></div><div style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">Experimenting is the name of this Long Covid game. Because the anxiety is close to gone, and the depression is improved (all due to ongoing medication) we are now turning our attention toward the very high Fatigue and his Sleep Onset delays.</span></div></div><div style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">While the two may sound connected, they might not be.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><h1 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">Fatigue</span></h1><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Fatigue is not about sleepiness. It is not resolved by getting enough sleep. Mike\'s specific form of fatigue is about lack of motivation, both physical and mental.&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s possible that when the last of the depression resolves, that could help, but given that the depression is low (he reports as a 30) and the fatigue is high (he reports as a 70) it makes sense to not wait around for the depression to resolve. Heck, maybe the fatigue is even the cause of the depression rather than the result. Nobody has answers for this.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">There are many conventional and unconventional remedies and management strategies for fatigue, of which we are aware.&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">So far, Mike has been using the technique of "pacing" for the fatigue. And now here\'s what we\'re adding:</span></div><div><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">CBD</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Wellbutrin</span></li></ol><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div></div><div><h1 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">Sleep Onset (aka Sleep Initiation)</span></h1><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Once Mike\'s asleep, he stays asleep, and almost always gets a refreshing sleep. So it\'s important to understand that there\'s more than one type of insomnia. What Mike has is called "Sleep Onset" delays. This means that in spite of efforts to rejigger his sleep schedule, his brain wants to fall asleep around 3:30 am, and wake up around 11:30. This schedule is not sustainable over the long term, as it is not conducive to running a business or being in step with friends, family, and leisure.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">&nbsp;</span></div><span style="font-family: arial;">From the <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3501671/?fbclid=IwAR1xIfHpZeh93NfLoNksL6B8t6MTJBBym-lRk3KAhVv6N-I1-kZ3kyFpAcQ#B39">NIH</a>, "Sleep onset latencies are often longer in CFS/ME patients compared to healthy control subjects, suggesting that some patients may have difficulties initiating sleep. It has been reported that CFS/ME patients may have differential parasympathetic activity at sleep onset, which may contribute to delayed sleep latency."&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">He\'s tried many of the conventional strategies, although not all. And so far, the only remedy that works well is alcohol. Remarkably, the conventional wisdom that alcohol will cause sleep disruption has not been true for Mike. The alcohol works well for sleep onset, and then he sleeps well and wakes up refreshed. Because we were trying to address other, more impotant issues, the alcohol (2-3 drinks per night) has come in handy, and with the endorsement of the doctor.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">But now that the anxiety and depression are largely addressed, we feel we can more directly address the sleep onset. And anyway, we have to, because he\'s now on Wellbturin for the fatigue, which disallows alcohol.&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Just as with fatigue, there are many suggested remedies of which we are aware.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Here\'s what we\'re trying:</span></div><div><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">CBD</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">A weighted blanket</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Melatonin</span></li></ol><span style="font-family: arial;">We\'re not yet addressing the brain fog directly. Maybe that\'s next or maybe it will resolve over time or due to other medications.&nbsp;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">\nComments? text-align: center;"><a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhFZGmkDIpTC5XPOIQl8y5IZjYkOpx7cTvw_x8JS3dSuU5Mm4bt1ng-P4E7hZvpOfsZd3jws4XQshjC4l_8k0LcCC3safFGFvdIkPTnecJqyBnkm94eNU0pr--J_1zVXIA2n1GwWrl1RNoYUmBstUEsMdWg-uFGL6seIdLoJAHjAqiRp4RNTrSQS1Cdsw/s225/download.jpg" style="clear: right; float: right; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: 1em;"><img border="0" data-original-height="225" data-original-width="225" height="225" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhFZGmkDIpTC5XPOIQl8y5IZjYkOpx7cTvw_x8JS3dSuU5Mm4bt1ng-P4E7hZvpOfsZd3jws4XQshjC4l_8k0LcCC3safFGFvdIkPTnecJqyBnkm94eNU0pr--J_1zVXIA2n1GwWrl1RNoYUmBstUEsMdWg-uFGL6seIdLoJAHjAqiRp4RNTrSQS1Cdsw/s1600/download.jpg" width="225" /></a></div><span style="font-family: arial;">Where We\'ve Landed</span></h3><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">Mike has ME/CFS (once known as Chronic Fatigue Syndrome.) And now the medical community is saying that Long Covid (PASC) can lead to ME/CFS. It\'s pretty clear that Mike\'s ME/CFS is caused by his Long Covid.</span></div><div style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><h3 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">Brief History of Mike\'s illness</span></h3><p><span style="font-family: arial;">His PASC illness started in January 2022 and has had three phases so far, and we are hopeful that the fourth phase will be complete recovery.</span></p><p></p><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Jan (with two medication fails and no successes) into Aug:&nbsp;</span></li><ul><li><span style="background-color: #f4cccc; font-family: arial;">Anxiety high</span></li><li><span style="background-color: #cfe2f3; font-family: arial;">Depression medium with spikes</span></li><li><span style="background-color: #ffe599; font-family: arial;">Fatigue low</span></li></ul><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Aug (after starting Gabapentin) into Sept.</span></li><ul><li><span style="background-color: #cfe2f3; font-family: arial;">Depression high</span></li><li><span style="background-color: #ffe599; font-family: arial;">Fatigue medium or more noticeable</span></li><li><span style="background-color: #f4cccc; font-family: arial;">Anxiety low (yay Gabapentin!)</span></li></ul><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Sept into Oct: (after starting Luvox)</span></li><ul><li><span style="background-color: #ffe599; font-family: arial;">Fatigue high or more noticeable</span></li><li><span style="background-color: #cfe2f3; font-family: arial;">Depression medium-low (yay Luvox!)</span></li><li><span style="background-color: #f4cccc; font-family: arial;">Anxiety low&nbsp;</span></li></ul></ol><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Now in October, he has just started Wellbutrin for the fatigue. We are hopeful that all will resolve soon.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><h3 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">Eight Key Points about ME/CFS and Mike</span></h3><div style="text-align: left;"><ol><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It is not tiredness. Read about that on <a href="https://www.webmd.com/multiple-sclerosis/qa/what-is-the-difference-between-tiredness-and-fatigue#:~:text=Medically%20speaking%2C%20tiredness%20happens%20to,tiredness%20not%20relieved%20by%20sleep." target="_blank">WebMD</a>.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Patients with ME/CFS used to be told to use "trial and error" to achieve optimal balance between activity and rest. The theory was that too much activity can lead to PEM, while too little activity causes deconditioning (regression).</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">The deconditioning theory and its treatment with graded exercise has been disproven. Instead, the patient with ME/CFS should get as much rest as possible. See this&nbsp;<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC8544443/" target="_blank">NIH</a>&nbsp;publication.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Therefore, what Mike needs is energy conservation strategies, such as pacing, which we\'ve been referring to as "spoon theory" and he needs to minimize the stimuli to which he is sensitive. For him, that sensitiviy is limited to certain light and sound.&nbsp;</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Symptoms can vary from one patient to the next. Not all patients get all the symptoms. Mike does not have the other sensitivies, nor does he have the pain, night waking, or the lesser common symptoms. He does have these ME/CFS symptoms</span></li><ol><li class="TrT0Xe" style="list-style-type: disc; margin: 0px 0px 4px; padding: 0px;"><span style="font-family: arial;">impaired higher order thinking</span></li><li class="TrT0Xe" style="list-style-type: disc; margin: 0px 0px 4px; padding: 0px;"><span style="font-family: arial;">sleep onset insomnia (aka initiation insomnia)</span></li><li class="TrT0Xe" style="list-style-type: disc; margin: 0px 0px 4px; padding: 0px;"><span style="font-family: arial;">dizziness when getting up out of his chair too fast</span></li><li class="TrT0Xe" style="list-style-type: disc; margin: 0px 0px 4px; padding: 0px;"><span style="font-family: arial;">impaired memory and concentration</span></li><li class="TrT0Xe" style="list-style-type: disc; margin: 0px 0px 4px; padding: 0px;"><span style="font-family: arial;">difficulties with sleep onset</span></li><li class="TrT0Xe" style="list-style-type: disc; margin: 0px 0px 4px; padding: 0px;"><span style="font-family: arial;">taking a long time to recover after physical activity</span></li><li class="TrT0Xe" style="list-style-type: disc; margin: 0px 0px 4px; padding: 0px;"><span style="font-family: arial;">extreme lack of motivation and initiative</span></li><li class="TrT0Xe" style="list-style-type: disc; margin: 0px 0px 4px; padding: 0px;"><span style="font-family: arial;">exhaustion after daily tasks, such as taking a shower</span></li></ol><li><span style="font-family: arial;">There is no cure; it has to be managed. Some patients do eventually recover; others have to manage it for the rest of their lives. Too little is known about neuro-PASC to able to predict probabilities for Mike.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">ME/CFS is still not well understood and is still vastly misunderstood.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">What is established is that viruses are known for leading to autoimmune diseases such as Epstein-Barr, ME/CFS, Herpes, Lupus and others. Here\'s a&nbsp;<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC6723519/table/viruses-11-00762-t001/?report=objectonly" target="_blank">list from the NIH</a>.&nbsp;&nbsp;</span></li></ol></div><p></p><h3 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">The Good News, if that\'s possible</span></h3><p><span style="font-family: arial;">The one up side of Long Covid\'s emergence as a contributor to autoimmune disease research is that it\'s pushing the medical community to re-evaluate ME/CFS (once known as Chronic Fatigue Syndrome) among many others. Read about this in <a href="https://www.theatlantic.com/health/archive/2022/09/mecfs-chronic-fatigue-syndrome-doctors-long-covid/671518/" target="_blank">The Atlantic</a>.&nbsp;</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Comments? Questions? Advice? So far, the sketchy evidence is that it probably won\'t last more than two years.&nbsp;</span><p></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Cognitive functioning has many components. It is granular. There is no way I would expect anyone to discern what\'s working and what\'s not working, just by spending a little time with Mike.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Indeed, there are only two people in total who we know can see some of these distinctions now, and they did need some&nbsp; details from me so they would know what to look for.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">And in fact, I might have missed some of these details myself, had it not been for raising a child with cognitive impairments.&nbsp;</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">I don\'t mean to draw similarities between Mike and Dave. What I do mean, though, is that cognitive function is more than one thing. It\'s dozens and dozens, which we had to see up close and learn about in order to best augment David\'s education.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">The following lists are for anyone who wants to really know what\'s happening with Mike. Or for anyone interested in cogntive functioning. Or for anyone wanting to learn about PASC. There are more, but this is enough to get the idea across.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Obviously I\'ve painted a black and white picture here, but doing so helps me to describe. Reality is a little more nuanced.</span></p><h2 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">What\'s not working</span></h2><div><span style="font-family: arial;">I need to point out here that when in public for short periods of time, he can appear to have some of these abilities. That is, he can "put on a good show." But he pays for it later in PEM (post exertional malaise.) Dr.&nbsp;Vijal&nbsp;Parikh says that Mike has loss of Executive Function and is on Sensory Overload</span></div><p></p><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Planning</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Motivation to do anything</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Making decisions</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Social Connection</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Problem-solving with providers on the phone</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Recall of what he\'s reading</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Emotional connection</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Initiating a conversation</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Initiating an idea</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Multitasking</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Watching television, except for game shows</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Short-term memory and long-term memory</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Cognitive flexibility</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Listening to music</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Divergent thinking</span></li></ol><h2 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">What\'s working</span></h2><div><ol><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Researching when directed to do so</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Working alone</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Problem-solving without outside help</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Answering questions</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Reading a physical book because he can read at his own pace and can reread as necessary</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">When he must be in public for short periods, he can appear "normal"</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Having the blinds open (finally)</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Convergent thinking</span></li></ol><div><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s no wonder people have a hard time believing how impaired he is. It\'s no wonder I have to write so much. It\'s no wonder that I, myself, have a hard time believing how impaired he is. There have been no changes in all these years, and we are reminded that many people are likely walking around with a brain mass without knowing it, and it has no effect. The only reason we even found out about it was an unrelated issue that warranted an MRI. They can sometimes change over time, which is why we had all those MRI\'s in the past.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He says there is no chance that the location of the tumor could cause anxiety, depression, or fatigue. He said that if the tumor was growing (it isn\'t) that it would cause numbness and weakness on the left side of my body. We are on the lookout for such symptoms.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">As he already said a year ago, no more MRI\'s are needed unless Mike gets the aforemention symtpoms.</span></li></ol><h3 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">The Psychiatrist:</span></h3><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Today I did not accompany Mike but I did send him along with my latest list of questions, plus some reading for Dr.&nbsp;</span><span style="font-family: arial;">Vijal&nbsp;</span><span style="font-family: arial;">Parikh. Results from today\'s visit:</span></div><span><span id="docs-internal-guid-7642e0b8-7fff-85eb-f154-73c59e49f85a"><p dir="ltr" style="background-color: white; line-height: 1.38; margin-bottom: 0pt; margin-top: 0pt;"></p><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;"><span style="background-color: transparent; color: #222222; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;">He read the article </span><a href="https://www.news-medical.net/news/20220921/Why-fatigue-from-long-COVID-goes-beyond-simply-feeling-tired.aspx" style="text-decoration-line: none;"><span style="background-color: transparent; color: #1155cc; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; text-decoration-line: underline; text-decoration-skip-ink: none; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;">https://www.news-medical.net/news/20220921/Why-fatigue-from-long-COVID-goes-beyond-simply-feeling-tired.aspx</span></a></span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Some of what was recommended in the article was experimental and not approved yet by the FDA, but he looked up a reference about use of an antibiotic to reduce brain inflammation and he wants to give that a try.&nbsp;</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">If that doesn\'t work, he wants to try Wellbutrin.&nbsp;</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He was not expecting the fatigue to get worse but says that\'s likely a direct result of Long COVID, rather than a symptom secondary to the anxiety or depression. He thinks that Mike shouldn\'t get any more tests or treatments except for maybe the antibiotics and Wellbutrin, because Mike will be seeing two Long COVID specialists* soon and because it might go away by itself by January.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He wants Mike to take the cannabis chocolate bars we obtained (CBD 20: THC 1) at night to see if that helps with the remaining depression and with sleep.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He agrees that Mike should sometimes fight the fatigue and sometimes rest depending on his judgement at the time. As my research has shown, fighting the fatigue can help heal the brain, but too much fighting can cause PEM (Post-exertional malaise&nbsp;) which can lead to a setback. So it\'s a balancing act.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He says that the inability to concentrate on watching TV and other things are about executive brain function and that the Wellbutrin might help with that or he might prescribe something that helps with ADD, because Mike\'s senses are being overwhelmed. But he wants to try other things first.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He says that Mike\'s ferritin levels (high or low) can be associated with fatigue but the therapeutic phlebotomies, which Mike\'s been getting for a few years, are helping with that.&nbsp;</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He is not concerned with any other explanations for fatigue until we run the course with the Long COVID. That includes all the chronic fatigue, anemia, etc.--he doesn\'t want to look into any of it yet. If the fatigue doesn\'t get better by January, we can start to look at deeper causes.</span></li></ol><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">* Mike has two upcoming appointments with Covid clinics. We made those appts many months ago because of long waiting lists. We had been hopeful we would no longer need them by now, but alas, we are glad we did not cancel.</span></div><div style="font-family: times;"><br /></div></span></span>'}, {'url': 'http://www.longcovidmind.info/2022/09/a-slightly-good-lateral-move.html', 'title': '22-09-20 A slightly good, lateral move', 'content': '<h3 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">An ironic discovery:</span></h3><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Even "one step forward, one step back" can constitute a kind of progress, of sorts.</span></p><h3 style="text-align: left;"><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjsCaqLfbvHmX63Xk-IWxN6e5et814it9LwgLvQcdxLT8O-JCan_upqR5ohklpKESTC5cfCMkVWAjiPKluFFC9tNKsXD155LpfZxxGtu4pNJMjw5PVgVnAobmYDmwAePZ4g_YpICkq1aSFha0okeb5yJR0D72e0uj12um1G6YBghwUwgyGxeNMjO8Bk3Q/s3268/img20220920_18552286.jpg" style="clear: right; float: right; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: 1em;"><span style="font-family: arial;"><img border="0" data-original-height="3268" data-original-width="2372" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjsCaqLfbvHmX63Xk-IWxN6e5et814it9LwgLvQcdxLT8O-JCan_upqR5ohklpKESTC5cfCMkVWAjiPKluFFC9tNKsXD155LpfZxxGtu4pNJMjw5PVgVnAobmYDmwAePZ4g_YpICkq1aSFha0okeb5yJR0D72e0uj12um1G6YBghwUwgyGxeNMjO8Bk3Q/s320/img20220920_18552286.jpg" width="232" /></span></a></div><span style="font-family: arial;">Ranking egregious symptoms:</span></h3><p><span style="font-family: arial;">None of us can compare symptoms or rank ourselves against someone else\'s symptoms. Yay! It often happens when he is seated, thankfully</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He can\'t think clearly and has no motivation. He has to force himself to do everything</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He can watch game shows, but can\'t watch any other television</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He keeps his blinds closed and can only read&nbsp;</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He can\'t move his body without a lot of forcing himself</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Dr.&nbsp;Vijal&nbsp;Parikh agrees that the fatigue is a direct result of Long Covid, and not secondary to the depression or anxiety</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Fatigue is common to Long Covid, and often occurs near the end of the illness, and so we are slightly encouraged</span></li></ol><h3 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">And a few more things to note:</span></h3><div><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">A few days ago he had an anxiety spike and it was actually life-related! We celebrate that kind of anxiety.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">After 20 mg of THC, the depression drops from 30 to zero. But of course, it doesn\'t last, and while it does, he\'s a zombie.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">He\'s getting ready to try the suggested 1:20 CBD to THC in the form of an edible we\'ve obtained, but he may wait until his next Dr. Parikh appt.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Alcohol is now helping again -- this time it\'s helping him fall asleep and get a refreshing sleep. That is, my energy is far more limited than it used to be. I conserve carefully, and I do go into the negative spoon count at times, which I pay for with odd symptoms such as brain fog, short-term memory loss, cooking mistakes, and general overwhelm</div><div><br /></div><div>Here\'s an article specifically about how&nbsp;<a href="https://angelmansyndromenews.com/health-insights/spoon-metaphor-in-angelman-syndrome/#:~:text=The%20spoon%20theory%20illustrates%20that,while%20caring%20for%20your%20child" target="_blank">Spoon Theory applies to caregivers of loved ones</a>&nbsp;with Angelman syndrome. But it applies to any caregiver of someone with chronic illness or disability.</div><div><br /></div><div>By conventional wisdom, getting help is important. But interestingly, some kinds of help require too many spoons.</div><div><br /></div><div>As caregivers, then, we need to expend a spoon or two, thinking through which kinds of help we can afford, in terms of the energy required to receive it.&nbsp;</div><div><br /></div><h3 style="text-align: left;">A Few Things I\'ve learned about Help</h3><div><ul style="text-align: left;"><li>If someone offers to bring me a meal, I ask them to please deliver it in something disposable so that I don\'t become responsible for returning it, which involves coordinating schedules.</li><li>If they want to visit, I think through whether their visit will involve accommodating their needs, as everyone has those. If it\'s too much for me, I decline the offer</li><li>I carefully consider any offer of help that requires a lot of back-and-forth scheduling, which can be draining</li><li>I turn down help from anyone who is in a hurry for things to "get back to normal." Their hearts are in the right place, but it\'s too much pressure on both of us</li><li>I\'ve learned to tell well-meaning listeners in advance that I don\'t need advice. That way I can then be selective, over time, with certain people who know my situation well, and then I can ask for advice for certain things&nbsp;</li><li>I\'ve given up looking for professional therapy. It\'s now harder to find than ever in this country, in part due to the burgeoning demands caused by the pandemic. I\'ve spent way too many spoons and far too much time seeking the help, filling out forms, working out insurance, then finding out their websites are out of date, and they don\'t have what I need. I now need therapy to help me recover from looking for therapy. So forget it</li></ul></div><div><br /></div><div>Helpers, loved ones, friends, are all well-meaning. In general, I ask myself if the help offered will have a net energizing effect. Even doing that requires spoons, but can be worth the effort, because support is important.</div><div><br /></div><div>What I need most is to be understood and to have Mike understood. And so, I write. It is energizing, knowing that I have some control over how people perceive Mike\'s illness, simply by writing. Oh and for those who want to advise me to "do something for myself," the blog-writing is for myself, and it is life-restoring.</div><div><br /></div><div>In contrast, the professional help community in this country, is not well-meaning, and I\'ve found the effort to not be worth the tradeoff of my energy and peace of mind. It\'s a mess and needs an overhaul.</div><div><br /></div><div>&nbsp;</div><div><br /></div><div>&nbsp;</div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">&nbsp;</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div>'}, {'url': 'http://www.longcovidmind.info/2022/09/spoon-theory.html', 'title': '22-09-16 Spoon Theory', 'content': '<p></p><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEghep7YJZUiovO3Qm9Cc12BeijyO7dBhVafiBo-PV3kiJKNxMKpXMB1KX0QEOztSCC60fd8t3j3d6KI95mx6_lq3Kp1Nyi6c5V-ZgC39uBvO9FnGL-Gr5LqQCf4meDi6TzcVNyICTJLYrEESTHEJnqw_gyV32FYyZ4IsY4spYqzNeWsc6ldTpzprB0Asw/s800/Spoon-theory.jpg" style="clear: right; float: right; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: 1em;"><img border="0" data-original-height="800" data-original-width="800" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEghep7YJZUiovO3Qm9Cc12BeijyO7dBhVafiBo-PV3kiJKNxMKpXMB1KX0QEOztSCC60fd8t3j3d6KI95mx6_lq3Kp1Nyi6c5V-ZgC39uBvO9FnGL-Gr5LqQCf4meDi6TzcVNyICTJLYrEESTHEJnqw_gyV32FYyZ4IsY4spYqzNeWsc6ldTpzprB0Asw/w320-h320/Spoon-theory.jpg" width="320" /></a></div><span style="font-family: arial;">People with energy-limiting illness or disability find themselves managing and counting their exertions every day. It\'s come to be known as Spoon Theory.</span><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">In brief, if you only had twelve spoons per day, and all efforts required one or more spoons, how would you spend them? Keep in mind that if you overspend, then you start tomorrow with fewer.<br /></span><div><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Mike has a chronic illness. It\'s time for us to think in terms of this pacing, whether we\'re happy about it or not. Nobody is happy about belonging to the Spoon Theory community. But it\'s helpful to know we\'re not alone.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">This picture was created by&nbsp;Christine Miserando, the originator of Spoon Theory and owner of&nbsp;<a href="https://butyoudontlooksick.com/articles/written-by-christine/the-spoon-theory/" target="_blank">But You Don\'t Look Sick</a></span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Her picture is a starting place. If I were to create a Spoon Theory chart specifically for Mike, it would look a little different from the above picture, as Mike is able to work. His limited spoons pertain almost entirely to keeping his business running.&nbsp;</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">According to <a href="https://longcovid.physio/" target="_blank">Long Covid Physio</a>, the type of pacing used differs between different health conditions.&nbsp;</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">As a reminder, his ability to work only partially reflects his "level" of illness. That is, we are in luck that the parts of his brain still functioning well are the problem-solving components, sans any kind of leadership, decision-making, or initiating.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">To the outside world, the practice of Spoon Theory is invisible. Keeping in mind that they do not live in the household, even close family can\'t possibly be cognizant all the time that their loved one is spending spoons at a rapid pace, and will be spoon-depleted or even spoon-negative in the privacy of their own home.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">As it applies to Mike, it\'s mostly just me and our son who can see it. We also have had the privilege of Mike spending extended time with two other people during all this, and they were able to see it too.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Others capable of understanding it, seeing it, or at least believing it, are others who also have been affected personally by the need for Spoon Theory.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">And still others who are open to reading and learning can understand it too.&nbsp; Thank you.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">And so here\'s where we are at concerning Mike\'s illness, which has somewhat turned a corner into more of a chronic illness and less a Covid-19 illness, even though its source is Covid-19, and we do have to keep that in mind as well, because studies are looking for the unique ways in which Covid-19 affects the brain in these extreme cases.</span></p><h3 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">About Spoon Theory</span></h3><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Spoon theory is recognized by <a href="https://www.webmd.com/multiple-sclerosis/features/spoon-theory">WebMD</a>, and applies to:</span></p><ul style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Dysautonomia</span></li><li><a href="https://www.webmd.com/multiple-sclerosis/features/spoon-theory"><span style="font-family: arial;">Multiple Sclerosis</span></a></li><li><a href="https://www.bjchealth.com.au/blog-fitness/the-spoon-theory-managing-fatigue-with-chronic-illness#:~:text=Spoon%20theory%2C%20uses%20spoons%2C%20as,a%20limited%20number%20of%20spoons."><span style="font-family: arial;">Chronic Fatigue syndrome</span></a></li><li><a href="https://health.clevelandclinic.org/spoon-theory-chronic-illness/#:~:text=%E2%80%9CThe%20spoon%20theory%20is%20a,who%20lives%20with%20chronic%20pain."><span style="font-family: arial;">Chronic Pain</span></a></li><li><a href="https://www.scarymommy.com/spoon-theory-chronic-illness"><span style="font-family: arial;">Lupus</span></a></li><li><a href="https://longcovid.physio/pacing"><span style="font-family: arial;">Long Covid</span></a></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Other illnesses such as cancer, HIV, fibromyalgia,&nbsp; COPD, and heart disease, and other disabilities</span></li></ul><h3 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">Some other terms</span></h3><div><ul style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Exertion intolerance</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Exertion limits</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Pacing</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Conserving energy</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Post Exertional Malaise</span></li></ul><h3 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">And one more thing</span></h3></div></div></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Spoon Theory applies to Caregivers, too</span></div>'}, {'url': 'http://www.longcovidmind.info/2022/09/one-step-forward-one-step-back.html', 'title': '22-09-14 A Step Forward, A Step Back', 'content': '<p></p><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEj9W8d7yDfOlmm07kXGUnFDigamilwcIwK1f0K_cfKECYP54J9Z0DpZGD37K27TMnixguV6rYfs54vQuqa0ui2S-6K3oWMJeFiCaKMmnfFCOgkR1xlfH8Wsc4TQ8VWZ3Y0AYW-s9kstTegneetETfi7q5cn8tT5nOEhF2kWEKpBtHVpZoUCIieZ24CgeQ/s225/optimist.png" style="clear: right; Regarded as the newest autoimmune-related disorder to emerge from the COVID-19 pandemic, this condition is being referred to as long-haul COVID or Post-Acute Sequelae of COVID (PASC)."</span></div><div style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">&nbsp;</span></div><div style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">What this means in all practicality:</span></div><div style="text-align: left;"><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s chronic, not acute</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s long-term</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It\'s hard to understand, as the patient can appear from the outside to be functioning, but those short-term public appearances take a heavy toll. So does any decision-making or leadership or trying to laugh</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">The "two steps forward and one step back" characterization, which certainly applies to many conditions, doesn\'t apply well here</span></li></ol><div><span style="font-family: arial;">A way to understand this is that the timeline is so slow that one can notice more granular trends than the common expression "two steps forward, one step back." After all, that\'s lumping two time periods together. Thus what we see from one week to the next, one month to the next, can consist of "one step forward, one step back." And sometimes it\'s the standalone "one step back" with the one step forward coming much later.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">This is hard for the outside world to understand -- even for those who have had illnesses which seemed maddeningly slow. To some extent, then, Long Covid is best understood by folks who\'ve had chronic, long-term auto-immune diseases or disabilities. Or someone close to them who\'ve been open to understanding it.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">So I need to tell our friends, loved ones, and support team something that may be hard to hear, but here goes -- it\'s almost better to think of Mike\'s illness as semi-permanent, as far as day-to-day living goes. That is, some of his issues might eventually resolve, some might improve somewhat, and some might have to be managed forever.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">That\'s not to say we are disallowing hope. We have to have hope. And we need optimism from those around us. You can even be Pollyannaish if it pleases you. But Mike and I are not being pessimistic. What we\'re doing is adjusting to the pace of this chronic illness. It resolves more slowly than is comfortable for those who love him. We all WANT him to get better sooner than that. We all WANT it to be two steps forward, one step back.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">Maybe if you can find it in your heart to accept Mike\'s illness as long-term, and stay with us day-to-day, well...that\'s a special kind of love. If you were to become a quadriplegic, would you want your loved ones to be waiting for you to get better? To become the old you? The analogy is approximate, but there\'s an element of similarity. This is so slow that he needs acceptance as he is.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">So yes, we\'re praying for the depression to lift. We\'re praying for the cognitive effects to lift. We\'re getting all the professional help. But regardless, he will never be the old Mike. He\'s already the new Mike. That\'s the only thing I\'m sure about. We are all adjusting to the new Mike.</span></div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div></div>'}, {'url': 'http://www.longcovidmind.info/2022/09/the-quest-for-learning-about-cbd-and-thc.html', 'title': '22-09-12 Figuring out CBD and THC ', 'content': '<h2 style="text-align: left;"><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: left;"><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEizDW7RnxUXXyMqH54HAeq9emp9kfpn9sszbsJXI94ImDPazBP5mgZwaM106SrdBHxSSldyoSyipxqWZXqi_AflpzUBhkcX_Ry28OAGZiBJVU1ti7E3-eVMBXej-WfRuMfJjrMAsmjx9TiNOteY_fLeE_hJx-JXAIxnVZ_fhYyrGSz0V1YHU_vd_ZZ2Dg/s627/chocolate-bar-marijuana-leaf-narcotic-sweets-isolated-vector-illustration-white-background-116677392.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" data-original-height="627" data-original-width="384" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEizDW7RnxUXXyMqH54HAeq9emp9kfpn9sszbsJXI94ImDPazBP5mgZwaM106SrdBHxSSldyoSyipxqWZXqi_AflpzUBhkcX_Ry28OAGZiBJVU1ti7E3-eVMBXej-WfRuMfJjrMAsmjx9TiNOteY_fLeE_hJx-JXAIxnVZ_fhYyrGSz0V1YHU_vd_ZZ2Dg/s320/chocolate-bar-marijuana-leaf-narcotic-sweets-isolated-vector-illustration-white-background-116677392.jpg" width="196" /></a></div><br /><span style="font-family: arial;">What Dr. Parikh suggested we find:&nbsp;</span></div></h2><p><span style="font-family: arial;">Dr.&nbsp;Vijal&nbsp;Parikh wants Mike on a 10 to 1 ratio of CBD to THC. Unfortunately, Dr. Parikh\'s hands are tied, as he is licensed to prescribe in New York, but Mike is a New Jersey resident. So we have to figure this out ourselves. He is being understandably careful about not taking the lead on this. But we can ask him questions.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">In New York, THC is by medical prescription only, but in New Jersey, we can get THC recreationally. That sounds good on paper, but those dispensaries are hard to find, and this particular order from the doctor may not be easy to find either.&nbsp;&nbsp;</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">We already have the THC, so we bought a bottle of CBD tincture. After arranging the right ratio and trying one night, Mike had a severe allergic reaction to the CBD. So now we have even more work to do.</span></p><p><span style="font-family: arial;">We put out a question to the universe and received two possible causes of the allergic reaction. With thanks to a few people and Google, here\'s what we understand so far:</span></p><p></p><ol style="text-align: left;"><blockquote><li><span style="font-family: arial;">&nbsp;It can be an allergic reaction to the CBD itself. Because it\'s a plant, it could have pollen, thus causing a reaction similar to seasonal allergies, which Mike does have, although they are largely controlled right now by Zertec. Pollen is not likely to be in CBD oil, as CBD is derived from the female plant, which does not have pollen</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">It can be caused by the additives which exist in broad or full spectrum CBD oil. It allows CBD to bind to something (as it does not have its TCH to bind to.) These additives are called terpenes, which exist in broad or full spectrum CBD oil. Examples of terpenes are linalool and D-limonene. Terpene allergies are rare. Sometimes terpenes even get added back to the singular kind, too, to "enhance" the effect.</span></li><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Here\'s more reading: https://royalcbd.com/allergic-reaction-to-cbd-oil/</span></li></blockquote></ol><h2 style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-family: arial;">Stores, head shops, dispensaries I visited in person:</span></h2><p></p><ol style="text-align: left;"><li><span style="font-family: arial;">Name: Redi Recreational Cannabis Store<br />Address: 111 Town Square, Jersey City<br />What I learned: It does not exist. They were caused by earlier failed medication attempts.</span></li></ol><div><span style="font-family: arial;">To see all the emails past and present online, or explore more writings about Mike\'s condition, I have now published a blog, which I continue to add to:</span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;"><a data-saferedirecturl="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.longcovidmind.info/&amp;source=gmail&amp;ust=1662389377186000&amp;usg=AOvVaw1a4gBvYKzTjnGJdvTI8GIk" href="https://www.longcovidmind.info/" rel="noreferrer" style="color: #1155cc;" target="_blank">Long Covid Mind</a></span></div><div><span style="font-family: arial;">&nbsp;</span></div></div></div></div></div>'}, {'url': 'http://www.longcovidmind.info/2022/09/mikes-visits-to-nyc.html', 'title': "22-09-01 Mike's visits to NYC", 'content': '<p></p><div class="separator" style="clear: both;
